% ===========================================================================
\section{Scaling Extrapolation to Production Parameters}\label{sec:validity}
% ===========================================================================

The benchmark runs $N=4$ workers on a 2-vCPU GitHub Actions runner,
which is far from the target deployment scale of $N=256$ workers and
$H=114{,}688$ (\hyperref[chap:formal_model]{the Formal Model}). We
stress that $N=256$ is an assumed target configuration for this
extrapolation, not a value observed on any production deployment or
hardcoded in the scheduler itself ($N$ is a runtime parameter). We
extrapolate using the theoretical scaling:

\begin{align}
  C_{\max}^{\mathrm{prod}}
  &\;\approx\; \frac{M \cdot \hat\delta_{\mathrm{sched}}^{\mathrm{DTA}}}{N_{\mathrm{prod}}}
  = \frac{M \times 400\,\mathrm{ns}}{256}
  = M \times 1.56\,\mathrm{ns}, \\
  C_{\max}^{\mathrm{Tokio,prod}}
  &\;\approx\; \frac{M \times 2000\,\mathrm{ns}}{256}
  = M \times 7.8\,\mathrm{ns}.
\end{align}
For the production benchmark of $M = 10^6$ tasks:
$C_{\max}^{\mathrm{DTA}} \approx 1.56\,\mathrm{ms}$
vs.\ $C_{\max}^{\mathrm{Tokio}} \approx 7.8\,\mathrm{ms}$ (extrapolated).

\begin{remark}[Caveats on extrapolation]
The above assumes perfect linear scaling in $N$, which is optimistic:
NUMA effects, inter-socket SPSC latency, and OS scheduler interference
will increase $\hat\delta_{\mathrm{sched}}$ for large $N$.
The theoretical $O(1/N)$ pair-correlation correction from
\hyperref[chap:kinetic_theory]{Performance~II} (Level~1 correction, $\Delta p_d \approx
-\varrho_0 p_d^2/(N-1)$) is negligible at $N=256$ and does not affect
the scaling estimate.
\end{remark}

\bigskip
\noindent\textbf{Summary.}
The Criterion benchmarks confirm the core theoretical predictions:
DTA's SPSC-based push architecture outperforms \tokio{}'s
WS-based pull architecture by 2.3--6.2$\times$ (spawn and deflection
micro-benchmarks combined; 2.6--6.2$\times$ for spawn alone) on the BoT
workloads for which it is designed.
The yield-latency regression ($4.7\times$ slower) is consistent with
DTA's stated design boundaries and does not affect BoT performance.
The CAS-storm paradox is empirically confirmed: DTA is fastest precisely
where WS suffers most (near-zero load), due to the architectural elimination
of idle-thread spinning.
All linear-scaling and overhead-dominance predictions from the formal
analysis match within the Criterion 5\% noise threshold for $M \ge 100\mathrm{k}$.
Moment analysis further shows that observed CI runner variance is
847--433{,}000$\times$ larger than the Gumbel-predicted fluctuation,
and that Tokio's CV plateau at large $M$ is a signature of allocator
contention not captured by the DTA mean-field model. Taken together with
the adversarial competitive-ratio bound, this part shows that DTA's
bounded-information routing is not just asymptotically competitive on
paper: it is measurably faster in practice, which is precisely the
empirical puzzle Part~VI's information-cost framework sets out to
explain.

% ============================================================
\clearpage
\partbreak
\part{Information-Theoretic Analysis of Scheduler Cost}
\clearpage

\bigskip
\noindent\textbf{Overview.}
The preceding parts establish that DTA's mean-field steady state, mixing
time, and adversarial makespan bound are broadly comparable to those of
Work Stealing (WS).  This part asks a sharper question: \emph{why, then,
does \dtact{} outperform \tokio{} by roughly 2--6$\times$
(precisely, 2.3--6.2$\times$; \S\ref{chap:empirical}) in practice?}
We argue that the conventional competitive-ratio criterion conflates two
orthogonal quantities --- how much a scheduler \emph{knows} about the
system, and how much it \emph{pays} to know it --- and introduce an
information-theoretic framework that separates them.  We prove that both
of these quantities tend to their best possible value for DTA as total
work $W \to \infty$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:dta_info_opt} and
Corollary~\ref{cor:dta_info_gap}): DTA achieves the best possible
makespan given its information set, \emph{and} the cost of that
restricted information set itself vanishes relative to the fully-informed
oracle in the heavy-traffic limit. This asymptotic equivalence does
\emph{not} explain the throughput gap observed at moderate, fixed task
counts, so we turn to a second, non-asymptotic quantity: the
information-\emph{acquisition} cost paid per unit time. We show that on a
NUMA topology this acquisition cost is $\Omega(\log N)$ times lower for
DTA than for WS, at any fixed system scale. On a representative
dual-socket system this cost advantage, not the asymptotic makespan
comparison, explains the observed throughput gap at moderate to large
task counts.

% ============================================================
\section*{Information-Theoretic Analysis}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Information-Theoretic Analysis}
\label{chap:info_theory}
\setcounter{section}{0}

% ============================================================
\section{Scheduler Information Model}
\label{sec:info_model}
% ============================================================

\subsection{Information sets and staleness}

A \emph{distributed scheduler} on worker set $G$ assigns tasks to workers
based on locally observable quantities.  We formalise what each scheduler
can observe.

\begin{definition}[Information propagation delay]\label{def:tau}
Let $\tau_{gh} \geq 0$ be the time for a state update at worker $h$ to be
observable by worker $g$ (e.g.\ a cache-coherence propagation latency
or an SPSC round-trip).  Define the \emph{information cone} of worker $g$
at time $t$ as
\[
  \mathcal{L}^-(g,t)
  \;=\;
  \bigl\{(h,s) : s \leq t - \tau_{gh}\bigr\},
\]
the set of worker--time pairs whose state can influence $g$'s decision at $t$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Scheduler information set]\label{def:info_set}
The \emph{information set} of scheduler $\pi$ at time $t$ is
\[
  \mathcal{I}_\pi(t)
  \;=\;
  \bigl\{\sigma_h(s) : (h,s) \in \mathcal{L}^-(g,t),\;
         \text{and } (h,s) \text{ was actively observed by } \pi\bigr\}.
\]
Scheduling decisions of $\pi$ must be measurable with respect to
$\mathcal{I}_\pi(t)$.
\end{definition}

For the two schedulers studied here:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{DTA.} Worker $g$ observes its own queue length $q_g(t)$
        continuously, and receives asynchronous SPSC notifications from
        its designated peers with delay $\tau \leq \delta_{\mathrm{inter}}/v$
        (where $v$ is the bus bandwidth).
        Formally, $\mathcal{I}_g^{\mathrm{DTA}}(t)
        = \{q_g(t)\} \cup \{\text{SPSC messages received up to }t\}$.

  \item \textbf{WS.} Worker $g$ observes $q_g(t)$ and, upon each steal
        attempt, reads the tail pointer of victim $h$'s deque with latency
        $\tau_{gh}$.  Between steals, $h$'s state is unobserved.
        Formally, $\mathcal{I}_g^{\mathrm{WS}}(t)
        = \{q_g(t)\} \cup \{q_h(t_{\mathrm{steal}}) : h \text{ stolen from at } t_{\mathrm{steal}} \leq t\}$.
\end{itemize}

The key structural difference: DTA receives a \emph{continuous, low-cost
stream} of local neighbourhood information; WS acquires \emph{point
observations} of arbitrary workers at the price of a CAS operation per
observation.

\subsection{Causal ordering and contention}

Two concurrent observations of the same memory location create a
\emph{causal collision}: their serialisation order is determined by the
memory subsystem, not the scheduler.  WS steal attempts can collide when
multiple workers target the same victim; DTA's SPSC channels are
\emph{causally ordered by construction} (single producer, single consumer),
so no collision is possible.  This distinction drives the cost analysis
in \S\ref{sec:info_cost}.

% ============================================================
\section{Information-Constrained Optimality}
\label{sec:info_optimality}
% ============================================================

\subsection{Constrained optimum and decomposition}

\begin{definition}[Information-constrained makespan]\label{def:Cstar}
For a scheduler $\pi$ with information set $\mathcal{I}_\pi$, the
\emph{information-constrained optimal makespan} is
\[
  C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi)
  \;=\;
  \inf_{\tilde\pi\,\text{measurable w.r.t.}\,\mathcal{I}_\pi}
  \mathbb{E}\bigl[C_{\max}(\tilde\pi)\bigr].
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Two-factor decomposition]\label{thm:decomp}
For any scheduler $\pi$:
\[
  \frac{\mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\pi)]}{C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}}}
  \;=\;
  \underbrace{\frac{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi)}{C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}}}}_{\displaystyle\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\pi)}\;
  \times\;
  \underbrace{\frac{\mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\pi)]}{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi)}}_{\displaystyle\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\pi)},
\]
where $C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}}$ is the offline-optimal makespan.
We call $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\pi) \geq 1$ the \emph{information gap} (structural,
cannot be improved without enlarging $\mathcal{I}_\pi$) and
$\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\pi) \geq 1$ the \emph{utilisation ratio} (algorithmic,
measures how well $\pi$ exploits its information).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By definition, $C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi) \leq \mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\pi)]$
and $C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}} \leq C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi)$.
The identity follows from telescoping.
\end{proof}

The decomposition shifts the question from
``how close is $\pi$ to the oracle?''\ to
``(a) how much does $\pi$'s limited information cost it, and
(b) does $\pi$ make optimal use of what it knows?''
Both DTA and WS are judged on criterion (b) alone in \S\ref{sec:info_optimality_proof};
criterion (a) is analysed in \S\ref{sec:numa_concrete}.

\subsection{DTA is information-optimal}
\label{sec:info_optimality_proof}

We prove $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA}) \to 1$ under mild assumptions.

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:mmp}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\textup{A\arabic*})]
  \item\label{ass:ergodic}
        \textbf{[Ergodic workload.]}
        The arrival process is stationary ergodic with finite rate
        $\lambda$ and finite second moment; service times are i.i.d.\
        with finite mean $1/\mu$ and finite variance $\sigma^2 < \infty$;
        load $\rho = \lambda/(N\mu) < 1$.  (The M/M/1 model is a special
        case; the key property used is ergodicity and $\rho < 1$.)

  \item\label{ass:threshold}
        \textbf{[Threshold structure.]}
        For each worker $g$, the cost-optimal local admission policy
        --- acting only on $q_g$ --- is a monotone threshold policy:
        accept if $q_g \leq H_g^*$, deflect otherwise.
        This holds for \emph{any} GI/GI/1/$H$ queue with a convex,
        non-decreasing running cost (e.g.\ sojourn time); see
        Stidham~\cite{stidham1985} for M/M/1/$H$ and the extension via
        convexity of the value function.
        DTA's admission threshold is $H$, the per-worker local-queue
        high-water mark of \hyperref[chap:formal_model]{the Formal Model}
        (\emph{not} to be confused with the hop-count bound $h_{\max} =
        \lfloor N/2\rfloor$ of Definition~\ref{def:workers}, which
        governs how many times an already-deflected chunk may be
        relayed, not when a worker starts deflecting); the assumption
        reduces to $H \geq
        H_g^*$, which for the M/M/1/$H$ case ($H^* =
        \lceil\rho/(1-\rho)\rceil$) is equivalent to $\rho \leq H/(H+1)$.
        Since DTA's $H$ is large in every configuration used in this
        paper (production $H = 114{,}688$; benchmark/simulation
        $H \in [100, 200]$; see \S\ref{sec:validity}), this holds for
        $\rho$ up to at least $0.99$ even at the smallest simulated $H$,
        comfortably covering every load $\varrho_0 \in [0.6, 0.95]$ used
        in the benchmarks elsewhere in this paper --- independent of
        $N$.

  \item\label{ass:bounded_tau}
        \textbf{[Bounded mean staleness.]}
        Information staleness satisfies
        $\mathbb{E}[\tau_{gh}] \leq \bar\tau < \infty$ for all $g,h \in G$.
        (This is weaker than requiring a.s.\ bounded staleness;
        it holds whenever the observation rate is bounded below by
        $1/\bar\tau$.)
\end{enumerate}
\end{assumption}

\begin{remark}[On Assumption~\ref{ass:threshold} and DTA's hop bound]
We stress that Assumption~\ref{ass:threshold} constrains the
\emph{admission} threshold $H$ (a queue-depth quantity, in the hundreds
to hundreds-of-thousands), not the \emph{hop-count} bound $h_{\max} =
\lfloor N/2\rfloor$ (a dimensionless integer, at most $N/2$) --- an
earlier version of this manuscript conflated the two, which produced a
spurious $N$-dependent condition ($\rho \leq 1-2/N$) that fails at small
$N$ and does not correspond to anything in the DTA implementation. The
condition actually required, $\rho \leq H/(H+1)$, is on the
\emph{system load} $\rho$ relative to the admission threshold $H$, and
is essentially independent of $N$: since $H \geq 100$ in every
configuration this paper considers, the condition holds up to
$\rho \leq 0.99$ regardless of $N$, so
Assumption~\ref{ass:threshold} is satisfied at every load and worker
count used elsewhere in this paper, including the $N=4$ CI benchmark
configuration.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[DTA utilisation efficiency]\label{thm:dta_info_opt}
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:mmp}, for every $\varepsilon > 0$ there exists
$W_0$ such that for all total work $W \geq W_0$:
\[
  \eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA})
  \;=\;
  \frac{\mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\mathrm{DTA})]}{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}})}
  \;\leq\; 1 + \varepsilon.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We decompose the argument into three steps.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Step~1 (Local threshold optimality).}
Model the routing decision at each arrival epoch as a
\emph{partially observed Markov decision process} (POMDP):
observable state $q_g \in \{0,\ldots,H\}$, action space
$\{\mathrm{accept}, \mathrm{deflect}\}$, instantaneous cost
$c(q) = q$ (sojourn contribution, by Little's law~\cite{little1961}).
The function $c$ is convex and non-decreasing.
For any stable single-server queue with convex running cost observed
through its queue length alone, the optimal policy is a
\emph{threshold policy}~\cite{stidham1985}: accept iff $q \leq H^*$.
By Assumption~\ref{ass:threshold}, DTA sets $H \geq H^*$, so its
threshold-$H$ policy achieves the minimum expected cost within
$\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}$:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:dta_local_opt}
  \mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\mathrm{DTA}) \mid \mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}]
  \;=\;
  C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}) \;+\; \Delta_{\mathrm{mf}},
\end{equation}
where $\Delta_{\mathrm{mf}}$ is the mean-field approximation error.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Step~2 (Mean-field error vanishes).}
By Assumption~\ref{ass:ergodic} and Theorem~\ref{prop:mf-recovery},
the steady-state queue-length distribution converges to the mean-field
fixed point $q^*$ with error $O(1/N)$ in total variation as $N \to \infty$.
By Assumption~\ref{ass:bounded_tau}, information staleness introduces
an additive bias $O(\bar\tau \cdot \lambda)$ to the effective arrival
rate seen by each worker, which is $o(1)$ for $\bar\tau \lambda \to 0$.
Under these conditions:
\[
  \Delta_{\mathrm{mf}} \;=\; O\!\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{W}}\right)
  \quad\text{as } W \to \infty,
\]
by the Gumbel CLT (Theorem~\ref{thm:fj_first}), since makespan
fluctuations scale as $O(W^{-1/2})$ relative to the mean.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Step~3 ($\eta_{\mathrm{util}} \to 1$).}
The oracle makespan satisfies
$C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}) = \Theta(W/(N\mu))$.
From~\eqref{eq:dta_local_opt}:
\[
  \eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA})
  \;=\;
  \frac{\mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\mathrm{DTA})]}{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}})}
  \;=\;
  1 + \frac{\Delta_{\mathrm{mf}}}{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}})}
  \;=\;
  1 + O\!\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{W}} \cdot \frac{N\mu}{W/(N\mu)}\right)
  \;=\; 1 + O(W^{-1/2}),
\]
which tends to~$1$ as $W \to \infty$, establishing the theorem for
every $\varepsilon > 0$ by choosing $W_0 = O(\varepsilon^{-2})$.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[The information gap also vanishes for DTA]
\label{cor:dta_info_gap}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:cr_bot_worst}
($\varrho_0 < 1$), $C_{\max}^{\mathrm{OPT}} = C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}}$
(the clairvoyant offline optimum of Definition~\ref{def:Cstar} and the
$C_{\max}^{\mathrm{OPT}}$ of \S\ref{chap:competitive_ratio} denote the
same quantity), so
\[
  \eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA}) \;\leq\; 1 + \frac{N\lfloor N/2\rfloor\,\delta_{\mathrm{hop}}}{W}
  \;\xrightarrow{W\to\infty}\; 1.
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
By Theorem~\ref{thm:decomp}, $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA})\cdot
\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA}) = \mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\mathrm{DTA})]/
C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}}$. Theorem~\ref{thm:cr_bot_worst} is a
\emph{pathwise} (deterministic, worst-case-over-$\sigma$) bound on
$C_{\max}^{\mathrm{DTA}}(\sigma)/C_{\max}^{\mathrm{OPT}}(\sigma)$, so it
also bounds the expectation of this ratio under the stationary ergodic
arrival process of Assumption~\ref{ass:mmp}(\ref{ass:ergodic}), giving
$\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA})\cdot\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA})
\leq 1 + N\lfloor N/2\rfloor\delta_{\mathrm{hop}}/W$. Since
$\eta_{\mathrm{info}}, \eta_{\mathrm{util}} \geq 1$ by definition (Theorem~\ref{thm:decomp}),
dividing by $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA}) \geq 1$ can only decrease
the bound, so $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA}) \leq
\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA})\cdot\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{DTA})
\leq 1 + N\lfloor N/2\rfloor\delta_{\mathrm{hop}}/W$, and the claim
follows since $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{DTA}) \geq 1$ always.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Scope of Corollary~\ref{cor:dta_info_gap}]
This closes the gap left open by Theorem~\ref{thm:dta_info_opt} alone:
together, the theorem and corollary show that \emph{both} factors of the
two-factor decomposition tend to $1$ for DTA as $W \to \infty$, so DTA is
information-optimal in the strong sense (matches the fully-informed
offline oracle), not merely utilisation-optimal within its own
information set. This is a large-workload (heavy-traffic) asymptotic
statement, consistent with the regime in which
Theorem~\ref{thm:cr_bot_worst} and Theorem~\ref{thm:dta_info_opt} are
themselves proved; it does not by itself imply anything about the
finite-$W$, finite-$N$ comparison against Work-Stealing, which is why
the acquisition-rate analysis of \S\ref{sec:info_cost}--\ref{sec:numa_concrete}
remains the operationally relevant comparison at fixed, moderate scale.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[WS also achieves $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}\to 1$]
\label{rem:ws_opt}
WS achieves $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{WS})\to 1$ by a
\emph{different} argument, since WS's information set
$\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}$ does not include individual queue lengths.
We argue in two parts.

\noindent\textbf{(a)~Random-victim optimality within $\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}$.}
At steady state, the non-idle workers' queue lengths form an
\emph{exchangeable} sequence: by symmetry of the mean-field dynamics
(Proposition~\ref{prop:mf-recovery}), all non-idle workers are
identically distributed.  Conditioned on $\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}$
(which contains no per-worker queue information), the marginal
distribution of $q_i$ is the same for every non-idle worker~$i$.
By de Finetti's theorem~\cite{norris1997}, any exchangeable prior
is a mixture of i.i.d.\ distributions, so for any Borel set $A$:
\[
  \mathbb{P}(q_i \in A \mid \mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}})
  \;=\;
  \mathbb{P}(q_j \in A \mid \mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}})
  \quad\text{for all non-idle }i,j.
\]
Therefore the expected steal yield $\mathbb{E}[q_{\mathrm{victim}} \mid
\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}]$ is \emph{identical} for every victim choice;
random-victim selection is Pareto-optimal (no deterministic policy
within $\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}$ dominates it)~\cite{blumofe1999}.

\noindent\textbf{(b)~Makespan efficiency.}
Under the $\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}$ constraint, WS achieves
$\mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\mathrm{WS})] \leq T_\infty + T_1/N$~\cite{blumofe1999},
where $T_\infty$ is the critical-path length and $T_1 = W/\mu$ is the
total work.  For bag-of-tasks workloads ($T_\infty \ll W/(N\mu)$),
this gives $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\mathrm{WS}) \leq 1 + T_\infty N\mu/W
\to 1$ as $W \to \infty$.

By the same squeeze argument as Corollary~\ref{cor:dta_info_gap}
(using $C_{\max}^{\mathrm{oracle}} \geq T_1/N$ and part~(b) above in place
of Theorem~\ref{thm:cr_bot_worst}), $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}(\mathrm{WS})
\leq 1 + T_\infty N\mu/W \to 1$ as well. So, exactly as for DTA, WS's
information gap also vanishes in the heavy-traffic limit --- this is
expected, since \emph{any} work-conserving BoT scheduler recovers the
oracle to leading order once $W$ dominates the fixed routing/stealing
overhead.

Hence \emph{both} DTA and WS achieve $\alpha \to 1$ and, by
Corollary~\ref{cor:dta_info_gap} and the argument just given,
$\eta_{\mathrm{info}} \to 1$ for both, in the $W \to \infty$ limit. The
asymptotic decomposition therefore does \emph{not} distinguish the two
schedulers; what does distinguish them are (i) the \emph{rate} at which
each ratio approaches $1$ --- governed by $N\lfloor N/2\rfloor\delta_{\mathrm{hop}}$
for DTA versus $T_\infty N\mu$ for WS, which are generally incomparable
without further workload assumptions --- and (ii) the information
acquisition rate $\beta$ each scheduler pays \emph{per unit time} to
attain its information set, analysed for finite, fixed system scale in
\S\ref{sec:info_cost}--\ref{sec:numa_concrete}. It is this second,
non-asymptotic cost comparison, not the heavy-traffic limit above, that
is the paper's substantive claim about why DTA outperforms
work-stealing in practice.
\end{remark}

% ============================================================
\section{Information Acquisition Rate}
\label{sec:info_cost}
% ============================================================

\subsection{Continuous rate function}

We model information acquisition as a \emph{cost flow}.

\begin{definition}[Information acquisition rate]\label{def:beta}
The \emph{information acquisition rate} of scheduler $\pi$ is the
stationary cost per unit time incurred in obtaining the observations that
constitute $\mathcal{I}_\pi$:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:beta_def}
  \beta_\pi
  \;=\;
  \lim_{T\to\infty}
  \frac{\mathcal{C}_\pi(T)}{T},
  \qquad
  \mathcal{C}_\pi(T)
  \;=\;
  \int_0^T \dot{\mathcal{C}}_\pi(t)\,\mathrm{d}t,
\end{equation}
where the instantaneous rate is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:cdot}
  \dot{\mathcal{C}}_\pi(t)
  \;=\;
  \sum_{g \in G}
  \Bigl[
    r_{\mathrm{SPSC}}^g(t)\cdot c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}
    \;+\;
    r_{\mathrm{CAS}}^g(t)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}\bigl(n_{\mathrm{conc}}^g(t)\bigr)
  \Bigr],
\end{equation}
with $r^g_{\cdot}$ the per-worker event rates and $n_{\mathrm{conc}}^g$
the number of workers concurrently competing for the same cache line as $g$.
\end{definition}

\subsection{Discrete-to-continuous correspondence}

In hardware terms, each observation is a discrete event: an SPSC
read costs $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$ cycles; a CAS costs
$c_{\mathrm{CAS}}(n_{\mathrm{conc}})$ cycles depending on contention.
The correspondence is:
\[
  \mathcal{C}_\pi(T)
  \;=\;
  \#_{\mathrm{SPSC}}(T)\cdot c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}
  \;+\;
  \sum_{\text{CAS event }k} c_{\mathrm{CAS}}(n_k),
\]
where $\#_{\mathrm{SPSC}}(T)$ counts SPSC reads in $[0,T]$ and $n_k$
is the concurrency at CAS event $k$.
Dividing by $T$ and taking the limit recovers $\beta_\pi$
from~\eqref{eq:beta_def}.

\subsection{CAS contention lower bound}

\paragraph{Model (Slotted CAS contention).}
Time is divided into hardware \emph{slots} of duration $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$
(one cache-coherence round-trip).  In each slot:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*),nosep]
  \item Each of the $m$ remaining workers independently attempts CAS
        with probability $p \in (0,1)$.
  \item If \emph{exactly one} worker attempts, the CAS succeeds for that
        worker (it exits the contention).
  \item If \emph{zero} workers attempt, the slot is idle; no state changes.
  \item If \emph{two or more} workers attempt simultaneously, the cache
        controller serialises them but all see a conflicting value
        and fail; each failed worker independently retries in a later slot
        according to its backoff schedule.
\end{enumerate}
Binary exponential backoff (BEB) sets $p = 2^{-k}$ after $k$ consecutive
failures.  After $r = \lfloor\log_2 n\rfloor$ rounds, all workers have
accumulated $r$ failures and transmit at rate $p_r = 2^{-r} \in [1/(2n), 1/n]$:
the \emph{steady-state regime}.  We analyse this regime directly.

\begin{lemma}[CAS contention cost]\label{lem:cas_contention}
Under the Slotted CAS model with $n$ workers transmitting at rate
$p = 1/n$ (the BEB steady-state rate), the expected per-worker
completion time satisfies
\begin{equation}\label{eq:cas_lb}
  H_n \cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}
  \;\leq\;
  E[T_{\mathrm{worker}}]
  \;\leq\;
  e \cdot H_n \cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)},
\end{equation}
where $H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k = \Theta(\log n)$.
In particular, $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}(n) = \Theta(\log n)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$,
and the \emph{lower bound} $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}(n) \geq H_n\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}
= \Omega(\log n)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$ holds.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Let $T_{m\to m-1}$ denote the number of slots to reduce the active
count from $m$ to $m-1$.  Given $m$ active workers each transmitting
with probability $p = 1/n$, a slot is successful (exactly one transmits)
with probability
\[
  p_m
  \;:=\;
  \binom{m}{1}\frac{1}{n}\!\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right)^{\!m-1}
  \;=\;
  \frac{m}{n}\!\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right)^{\!m-1}.
\]
Since $T_{m\to m-1}$ is geometrically distributed with success
probability $p_m$, we have $\mathbb{E}[T_{m\to m-1}] = 1/p_m$.

\noindent\textbf{Lower bound.}
The inequality $(1-1/n)^{m-1} \leq 1$ gives $p_m \leq m/n$, hence
\[
  \mathbb{E}[T_{m\to m-1}]
  \;=\; \frac{1}{p_m}
  \;\geq\; \frac{n}{m}.
\]
Summing over $m = 1,\ldots,n$ and dividing by $n$:
\[
  \mathbb{E}[T_{\mathrm{worker}}]
  \;=\;
  \frac{1}{n}\sum_{m=1}^{n}\mathbb{E}[T_{m\to m-1}]
  \;\geq\;
  \frac{1}{n}\sum_{m=1}^{n}\frac{n}{m}
  \;=\;
  H_n.
\]

\noindent\textbf{Upper bound.}
For $1 \leq m \leq n$ and $n \geq 2$, the sequence $(1-1/n)^{m-1}$
is decreasing in $m$, so
$(1-1/n)^{m-1} \geq (1-1/n)^{n-1}$.
Since $(1-1/n)^{n-1} \geq 1/e$ for all $n \geq 1$
(the function $n\mapsto (1-1/n)^{n-1}$ decreases to $1/e$ from above),
we get $p_m \geq m/(en)$, hence
\[
  \mathbb{E}[T_{m\to m-1}]
  \;\leq\;
  \frac{en}{m}.
\]
Summing and dividing by $n$: $\mathbb{E}[T_{\mathrm{worker}}] \leq e\cdot H_n$.

\noindent\textbf{BEB overhead.}
The $\lfloor\log_2 n\rfloor$ rounds before the BEB steady-state add at
most $\lfloor\log_2 n\rfloor \cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)} = O(\log n)$
to the per-worker cost, which is absorbed into the $\Theta(\log n)$
bound without affecting the constants in~\eqref{eq:cas_lb}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Why the coupon-collector argument requires care]
A na\"\i{}ve application of the coupon-collector~\cite{mitzenmacher2017} to CAS conflates two
distinct models.  The coupon-collector (draw uniformly from $n$ items
\emph{with replacement} until all are drawn) applies when the hardware
\emph{resamples} from the entire pool every round, even after a worker
has succeeded --- a model without exit.  In the correct model (workers
\emph{exit} after success), the per-round success probability
$p_m = (m/n)(1-1/n)^{m-1}$ depends on $m$, and the tight bound
$H_n \leq \mathbb{E}[T_{\mathrm{worker}}] \leq e\cdot H_n$ follows from
the geometric-sum analysis above rather than from the coupon-collector
directly.
\end{remark}

% ============================================================
\section{DTA vs.\ WS: Abstract Comparison}
\label{sec:dta_ws_abstract}
% ============================================================

\begin{definition}[$(\alpha,\beta)$-information-competitive]\label{def:alpha_beta}
A scheduler $\pi$ is \emph{$(\alpha,\beta)$-information-competitive} if
\[
  \eta_{\mathrm{util}}(\pi) \leq \alpha
  \quad\text{and}\quad
  \beta_\pi \leq \beta.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[DTA vs.\ WS in the $(\alpha,\beta)$ plane]
\label{thm:alpha_beta}
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:mmp} and Lemma~\ref{lem:cas_contention},
with $\lambda_{\mathrm{steal}} \approx \lambda_{\mathrm{deflect}} \eqqcolon \lambda$
at high load:
\begin{align}
  \text{DTA:}\quad &
    \bigl(1+\varepsilon,\;
      N\lambda\cdot c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}\bigr)\text{-info-competitive},
      \label{eq:dta_ab}\\[4pt]
  \text{WS:}\quad &
    \bigl(1+\varepsilon',\;
      N\lambda\cdot\bigl(\bar\delta + \Omega(\log N)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}\bigr)\bigr)
      \text{-info-competitive},
      \label{eq:ws_ab}
\end{align}
for every $\varepsilon, \varepsilon' > 0$ as $W \to \infty$.
In particular,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:beta_ratio}
  \frac{\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}}{\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}}
  \;=\;
  \frac{\bar\delta + \Omega(\log N)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}}{c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}}
  \;=\; \Omega(\log N).
\end{equation}
Both schedulers achieve $\alpha \to 1$; the cost advantage of DTA
is $\Omega(\log N)$ in $\beta$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The $\alpha$ bounds follow from Theorem~\ref{thm:dta_info_opt} and its
Remark (WS analogue).
For DTA: each task triggers at most one SPSC message of cost $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$;
$r_{\mathrm{CAS}}^\mathrm{DTA} = 0$ (SPSC channels are contention-free), giving
$\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}} = N\lambda \cdot c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$.
For WS: each steal attempt reads the victim's deque tail (latency $\delta(g,h)$,
average $\bar\delta$) and executes a CAS with $n_{\mathrm{conc}} \leq N$
concurrent contenders; by Lemma~\ref{lem:cas_contention},
$c_{\mathrm{CAS}}(N) = \Omega(\log N)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$.
Hence $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}} = N\lambda(\bar\delta + \Omega(\log N)\cdot c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)})$.
The ratio~\eqref{eq:beta_ratio} follows since $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}} \leq \delta_{\mathrm{intra}} \leq \bar\delta$.
\end{proof}

% ============================================================
\section{Concrete Analysis on a Dual-Socket NUMA Topology}
\label{sec:numa_concrete}
% ============================================================

We instantiate the abstract framework on the simplest non-trivial NUMA
topology: a two-socket system with $N/2$ cores per socket.

\subsection{Topology and parameters}

Set $G = \mathbb{Z}_2 \times \mathbb{Z}_{N/2}$ (cf.~\S\ref{sec:future_work},
Open Problem~4).  The intra-socket subgroup is
$H = \{0\} \times \mathbb{Z}_{N/2}$.  We use the following representative
order-of-magnitude parameters for a modern dual-socket server (e.g.\
AMD EPYC 7763 or Intel Xeon Platinum 8380, both of which match this
profile); these are illustrative estimates drawn from published
hardware latency figures, not latencies measured on our own testbed:
\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\toprule
Parameter & Symbol & Typical value \\
\midrule
Intra-socket SPSC latency & $\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$ & $80\;\mathrm{ns}$ \\
Cross-socket NUMA latency & $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}$ & $300\;\mathrm{ns}$ \\
Uncontended CAS latency   & $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$ & $100\;\mathrm{ns}$ \\
SPSC read cost            & $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$       & $\delta_{\mathrm{intra}} = 80\;\mathrm{ns}$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The average NUMA latency is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:delta_bar_numa}
  \bar\delta
  = \delta_{\mathrm{intra}}\cdot\frac{N/2-1}{N-1}
  + \delta_{\mathrm{inter}}\cdot\frac{N/2}{N-1}
  \;\xrightarrow{N\to\infty}\;
  \frac{\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}+\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}}{2}
  = 190\;\mathrm{ns}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Information acquisition cost at concrete $N$}

Definition~\ref{def:beta} defines $\beta_\pi$ as a $T \to \infty$
long-run average rate. The closed-form expressions below evaluate this
limit at a fixed observation rate $\lambda$ and worker count $N$, using
the steady-state per-observation costs derived in
\S\ref{sec:numa_concrete}; this substitution is exact once the system
has reached stationarity (the $T \to \infty$ limit exists and equals the
stationary expectation by the ergodic theorem under
Assumption~\ref{ass:ergodic}), so no additional finite-$T$ approximation
error is introduced here. The only approximation is in the per-observation
cost constants themselves ($\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$, $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}$,
$c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$), which \S\ref{sec:numa_concrete} already flags
as order-of-magnitude, not measured, hardware figures.
From Theorem~\ref{thm:alpha_beta}:
\begin{align}
  \beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}(N) &= N\lambda\cdot 80\;\mathrm{ns}, \label{eq:beta_dta_num}\\
  \beta_{\mathrm{WS}}(N)  &= N\lambda\cdot\bigl(190 + 100\log_2 N\bigr)\;\mathrm{ns}.
  \label{eq:beta_ws_num}
\end{align}
The ratio $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}/\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}$ at representative core counts:
\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{rrr}
\toprule
$N$ & $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}/\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}$ & WS CAS overhead ($100\log_2 N$ ns)\\
\midrule
  8 & $\approx 6.3\times$ & 300 ns \\
 16 & $\approx 7.3\times$ & 400 ns \\
 32 & $\approx 8.3\times$ & 500 ns \\
 64 & $\approx 9.9\times$ & 600 ns \\
128 & $\approx 11.1\times$ & 700 ns \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
The cost ratio grows logarithmically with $N$, consistent with the
observed widening of the throughput advantage as task count grows
(cf.\ Table~\ref{tab:summary}).

\subsection{Information gap via Fourier modes}

Using the group Fourier decomposition~\eqref{eq:numa_fourier}
from Open Problem~4, with $G = \mathbb{Z}_2 \times \mathbb{Z}_{N/2}$
the only non-trivial inter-socket mode is $(k,\ell) = (1,0)$:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:info_gap_2socket}
  C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}) - C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}})
  \;\leq\;
  \frac{|\hat{F}_{(1,0)}|^2}{\mu N}
  \;=\;
  \frac{(\Delta q)^2}{4\mu N},
\end{equation}
where $\Delta q = |q_{\mathrm{socket\,0}} - q_{\mathrm{socket\,1}}|$ is the
inter-socket queue imbalance.  In the balanced BoT regime ($W \to\infty$):
$\Delta q = O(W^{-1/2}) \to 0$, so the information gap vanishes and
$C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}) / C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}}) \to 1$.

\subsection{Total cost and the crossover threshold $\gamma^*$}

\begin{definition}[Total scheduler cost]\label{def:J}
Let $\gamma \geq 0$ be a \emph{cost-weight} converting information
acquisition cost into makespan-equivalent units.  The
\emph{total cost} of scheduler $\pi$ over horizon $T$ is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:J}
  \mathcal{J}(\pi;\gamma)
  \;=\;
  \mathbb{E}[C_{\max}(\pi)]
  \;+\;
  \gamma\cdot\beta_\pi\cdot T.
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\noindent DTA dominates WS ($\mathcal{J}_{\mathrm{DTA}} < \mathcal{J}_{\mathrm{WS}}$)
if and only if
\begin{equation}\label{eq:gamma_star}
  \gamma \;>\; \gamma^*
  \;\triangleq\;
  \frac{C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{DTA}}) - C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}^{\mathrm{WS}})}{
        (\beta_{\mathrm{WS}} - \beta_{\mathrm{DTA}})\cdot T}
  \;\leq\;
  \frac{(\Delta q)^2}{4\mu N \cdot (\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}-\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}})\cdot T},
\end{equation}
where we used~\eqref{eq:info_gap_2socket} for the numerator.
Substituting~\eqref{eq:beta_dta_num}--\eqref{eq:beta_ws_num}
with $N=64$ and $\Delta q = O(1)$ (light unbalance):
\[
  \gamma^*
  \;\leq\;
  \frac{O(1)}{4\mu\cdot 64 \cdot 64\lambda(110+600)\,\mathrm{ns}\cdot T}
  \;=\; o(1/T).
\]
Since $\gamma^* \to 0$ as $W = \lambda T \to\infty$, \emph{DTA dominates
WS in total cost at any positive information weight $\gamma>0$ for
sufficiently large workloads.}

\subsection{Methodology for applying the framework}

We summarise a concrete procedure for practitioners choosing between
DTA and WS on a given target platform.

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Measure hardware parameters.}
        Obtain $\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$, $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}$,
        $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$, and $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$ via
        \texttt{lmbench} or \texttt{likwid-bench}.

  \item \textbf{Estimate workload parameters.}
        Measure arrival rate $\lambda$, mean service time $1/\mu$, and
        load $\rho$.  Estimate the steady-state inter-socket imbalance
        $\Delta q$ from a short profiling run.

  \item \textbf{Compute $\beta$ values.}
        Use~\eqref{eq:beta_dta_num}--\eqref{eq:beta_ws_num}
        (or the general formulas of \S\ref{sec:info_cost})
        with the measured parameters.

  \item \textbf{Compute $\gamma^*$.}
        Apply~\eqref{eq:gamma_star}.  If the application's natural
        $\gamma$ (e.g.\ latency penalty in SLA contracts, converted to
        ns) satisfies $\gamma > \gamma^*$: choose DTA.

  \item \textbf{Sensitivity analysis.}
        Plot $\mathcal{J}(\pi;\gamma)$ for both schedulers as $\gamma$
        varies.  The crossing point is $\gamma^*$; the margin
        $|\gamma_{\mathrm{app}} - \gamma^*|$ quantifies robustness to
        parameter uncertainty.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}[t]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figure/numa_info_cost.png}
  \caption{Information-acquisition cost analysis for DTA vs.\ WS on a
    dual-socket NUMA system
    ($\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}=80\,\mathrm{ns}$,
     $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}=300\,\mathrm{ns}$,
     $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}=100\,\mathrm{ns}$,
     $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}=80\,\mathrm{ns}$).
    \textbf{(A)}~Cost ratio $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}/\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}$ vs.\ $N$,
    confirming $\Omega(\log N)$ growth (Theorem~\ref{thm:alpha_beta}).
    \textbf{(B)}~Absolute acquisition costs per task; the shaded region is
    the DTA cost advantage.
    \textbf{(C)}~Total cost $\mathcal{J}(\pi;\gamma)$ for
    $W \in \{10^3, 10^4, 10^5\}$ tasks ($N=64$, $\Delta q=5$ tasks):
    DTA dominates above the respective crossover $\gamma^*$ (vertical lines).
    \textbf{(D)}~Relative information gap
    $\Delta C^*/C^*_{\mathrm{WS}}$ vs.\ total work $W$ under three
    imbalance models; the gap vanishes as $O(W^{-1/2})$ in the balanced
    regime (cf.~\eqref{eq:info_gap_2socket}).}
  \label{fig:numa_info_cost}
\end{figure}

\begin{remark}[Regime where WS may be preferred]
When load is low ($\rho \ll 1$, steal rate $\lambda_{\mathrm{steal}} \ll
\lambda_{\mathrm{deflect}}$), WS's steal events are rare and CAS contention
is light.  In this regime $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}} \approx \beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}$
and the information gap~\eqref{eq:info_gap_2socket} can be non-negligible
(imbalance $\Delta q$ is larger at low load).  The framework predicts
WS may outperform DTA for very light, unbalanced workloads --- consistent
with the theoretical scope characterised in \S\ref{sec:dta_scope}.
\end{remark}

\bigskip
\noindent\textbf{Summary.}
This part introduced an information-theoretic framework that resolves the
apparent paradox between DTA's limited global information and its empirically
observed throughput advantage over WS\@.
The key results are:
(i)~a two-factor decomposition of the competitive ratio into a structural
\emph{information gap} $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}$ and an algorithmic
\emph{utilisation ratio} $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}$;
(ii)~a proof that DTA achieves $\eta_{\mathrm{util}} \to 1$ as $W \to \infty$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:dta_info_opt}) --- utilisation-optimal within its
observation class --- together with $\eta_{\mathrm{info}} \to 1$
(Corollary~\ref{cor:dta_info_gap}), so DTA is information-optimal in the
full, heavy-traffic sense;
(iii)~both DTA and WS satisfy $\eta_{\mathrm{util}} \to 1$ \emph{and}
$\eta_{\mathrm{info}} \to 1$ in this same heavy-traffic limit, so the
asymptotic decomposition alone does not distinguish them; what does
distinguish them at fixed, moderate scale is the information acquisition
cost;
(iv)~$\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}/\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}} = \Omega(\log N)$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:alpha_beta});
(v)~on a 64-core dual-socket NUMA system, WS pays $\approx 9.9\times$ more
in acquisition cost per task, growing to $11\times$ at 128 cores;
(vi)~$\gamma^* \to 0$ as $W \to \infty$: DTA dominates total cost at any
positive information-cost weight for large workloads.

\clearpage
\partbreak
\part{Conclusions and Outlook}
\clearpage

\bigskip
\noindent\textbf{Overview.}
This part returns to the question posed in the Introduction
(\S\ref{sec:intro}): is restricting a scheduler to bounded, local
information a free design choice, or a real cost? We synthesise the
paper's answer, characterise the range of applicability of the developed
methods and the DTA algorithm, and identify the most promising directions
for future work. The information-theoretic cost framework developed in
Part~VI provides the analytical basis for the applicability conclusions
in \S\ref{sec:dta_scope}.

% ============================================================
\section*{Conclusions and Outlook}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Conclusions and Outlook}
\label{chap:conclusions}
\setcounter{section}{0}

% ============================================================
\section{Mathematical Methods: Applicability to Distributed Systems}
\label{sec:methods_scope}
% ============================================================

This paper applies three analytical frameworks: \emph{operator-algebraic
modelling}, \emph{kinetic-theory / BBGKY expansion}, and an
\emph{information-theoretic cost framework}.  The first two originated in
mathematical physics; the third draws on classical information
theory~\cite{shannon1948,cover_thomas2006} and queueing control
theory~\cite{stidham1985,weber1990}.  We sketch the scope of each.

\subsection{Operator-algebraic modelling}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Core idea.}
Any distributed system in which tasks (or messages, or tokens) move
between a finite set of \emph{sites} (queues, threads, buffers, nodes)
can be encoded in an occupation-number Fock space~\cite{fock1932}.
Each atomic state transition becomes a composition of creation and
annihilation operators.  Conservation laws --- the most common
correctness requirements --- then correspond to commutation relations
between the total number operator $\hat{N}$ and the transition
operators.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Applicability conditions.}
The method is exact (not an approximation) whenever:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The state space is finite and discrete (as in any real implementation).
  \item Tasks (tokens) are distinguishable and subject to a capacity constraint.
  \item Correctness is stated as a conservation or reachability property.
\end{enumerate}
It is therefore applicable, without modification, to: message-passing
middleware (RPC queues, Kafka topics as sites), actor-model runtimes
(each actor mailbox is a site), connection-pool schedulers, workflow
engines with explicit task lifecycle states, and lock-free data structure
verification.  The only requirement is that the state graph be expressible
as a finite-capacity site algebra.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Advantages over traditional model checking.}
Model checkers (e.g.\ TLA+, Loom~\cite{loom2023}) verify correctness by state-space
exploration, which is exponential in the number of tasks $P$.
The operator-algebraic proof is \emph{size-independent}: the commutation
identity $[\hat{N}, \hat{T}_{\alpha\to\beta}] = 0$ is proved once and holds
for all $P$.  This makes the method well-suited to systems where the task
count is not bounded a priori.

\subsection{Kinetic-theory / BBGKY hierarchy}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Core idea.}
In any system with $N \gg 1$ interacting agents (workers, nodes, replicas)
where the interaction is \emph{pairwise} and \emph{symmetric} (any worker
may interact with any other), the $N$-body dynamics admits a systematic
expansion in powers of $1/N$.  The zeroth-order term is a mean-field (MF)
ODE or master equation; the first-order term captures pairwise correlations
via a pair-density correction; and so on.  This is precisely the BBGKY
hierarchy~\cite{bogoliubov1946,born_green1949,kirkwood1946,yvon1935},
familiar from kinetic gas theory.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Transfer recipe for distributed systems.}
Given a distributed system with $N$ symmetric agents:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Identify the one-body density.}
        Let $f_1(q, t)$ be the marginal distribution of agent state
        (e.g.\ queue length, buffer occupancy, CPU load).
  \item \textbf{Write the exact evolution equation for $f_1$.}
        This will depend on the two-body density $f_2(q, q', t)$
        (correlation between two agents), giving BBGKY level 1.
  \item \textbf{Apply the Stosszahlansatz ($N \to \infty$).}
        Factorise $f_2 \approx f_1 \otimes f_1$.  The result is a
        closed MF equation (an ODE or PDE) that is exact as $N \to \infty$.
  \item \textbf{Compute the level-1 correction.}
        Write $f_2 = f_1 \otimes f_1 + g_2$ and derive the ODE for
        $g_2$ from the level-2 BBGKY equation, closing by setting
        $g_3 \approx 0$.  This gives an $O(1/N)$ correction to the MF.
  \item \textbf{Validate numerically.}
        Run a CTMC simulation at finite $N$ and compare with the
        MF prediction and its $O(1/N)$ correction.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Applicability conditions.}
The method requires:
(i)~pairwise, symmetric interactions (no fixed master-worker hierarchy);
(ii)~a well-defined thermodynamic limit $N \to \infty$;
(iii)~ergodicity (the CTMC is irreducible under the mean-field closure).
It is directly applicable to: supermarket-model load balancers~\cite{mitzenmacher2001,vvedenskaya1996},
gossip protocols and epidemic spreading, distributed consensus
(Raft/Paxos with $N$ replicas), connection-pool rebalancing,
and any system analysed by the ``power of two choices'' framework~\cite{azar1999}.
The MF result is typically tight when $N \geq 8$; the Level-1 correction
is significant for small $N$ (4--8 agents), exactly the practically
relevant regime for multicore schedulers.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Extension to DAGs.}
As shown in Performance~V (\S\ref{sec:dag_kinetic}), the kinetic framework
extends naturally to heterogeneous workloads with dependency graphs:
the layer-completion fraction $f_\ell(t)$ plays the role of $f_1$, and
the fan-in $K$ controls the sharpness of the phase transition from
second-order (smooth, finite $K$) to first-order (discontinuous,
$K \to \infty$, pure fork-join).

\subsection{Information-theoretic cost framework}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Core idea.}
Any distributed scheduler makes decisions under \emph{limited information}
and incurs a cost to acquire that information.  Part~VI formalises this
observation by separating the total competitive ratio into two independent
factors: the \emph{information gap} $\eta_{\mathrm{info}}$ (how much the
scheduler's limited view of the system costs it, structurally) and the
\emph{utilisation ratio} $\eta_{\mathrm{util}}$ (how efficiently it uses
what it knows).  Information-acquisition cost $\beta_\pi$ is defined as a
continuous rate function over hardware observations (SPSC reads, CAS
operations), with a discrete-to-continuous bridge to actual cycle counts.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Applicability conditions.}
The framework applies to any pair of schedulers that differ in
(i)~the information set they observe, and
(ii)~the hardware cost of those observations.
It requires a queueing model (for $C_{\max}^*(\mathcal{I}_\pi)$) and
hardware latency measurements ($\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$,
$\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}$, $c_{\mathrm{CAS}}^{(0)}$, $c_{\mathrm{SPSC}}$).
It is directly applicable to any work-stealing vs.\ push-based scheduler
comparison on NUMA or multi-socket hardware.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Key output: the $\gamma^*$ crossover.}
The framework produces a concrete \emph{crossover threshold} $\gamma^*$:
for any positive information-cost weight $\gamma > \gamma^*$, DTA's lower
acquisition cost outweighs WS's information advantage, and DTA achieves
lower total cost.  At high load ($W \to \infty$), $\gamma^* \to 0$, so
DTA dominates at any positive $\gamma$.

% ============================================================
\section{DTA Algorithm: Applicability and Use-Case Scope}
\label{sec:dta_scope}
% ============================================================

The DTA scheduler is designed for and performs best in a specific
operational envelope.  We characterise this envelope using the
theoretical results of Parts~III--V.

\subsection{Workload types}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Best fit: Bag-of-Tasks (BoT) at moderate-to-high load.}
The competitive ratio analysis (Part~V, \S\ref{chap:competitive_ratio}) shows
$\rho_c^{\mathrm{DTA}} \to 1$ as total work $W \to \infty$ under the
mean-field assumption.  The adversarial worst-case bound
(\S\ref{sec:cr_bot_worst}) gives $\rho_c^{\mathrm{DTA,worst}} \leq
1 + N\lfloor N/2\rfloor\delta_{\mathrm{hop}}/W$, which is also $\to 1$
for large $W$.  BoT workloads at high load are therefore the primary
design target: the deflection overhead is amortised over task computation.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Good fit: Parallel batch computation.}
Any workload decomposable into a fixed pool of independent tasks
--- matrix-vector products, data-parallel map-reduce, Monte Carlo
simulation, image tile processing --- maps directly to the BoT model.
The spawn-efficiency benchmarks confirm 2.6--6.2$\times$ speedup over
Tokio at spawn task counts $\geq 1$k (Figure~\ref{fig:speedup}); the
wider 2.3--6.2$\times$ range quoted elsewhere in this paper additionally
includes the deflection micro-benchmarks (Defl-1k through Defl-10M),
whose speedup ratios span 2.3--4.2$\times$.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Moderate fit: Fork-join DAGs with bounded fan-in.}
The DAG kinetic theory (Performance~V, Theorem~\ref{thm:fj_first})
gives $E[C_{\max}] \leq T_1/N + d \cdot H_K/\mu$, which is tight
for moderate $K$ (fan-in).  For shallow DAGs ($d \leq 10$, $K \leq 16$)
the bound is near-optimal.  DTA's SPSC-push routing is efficient for
inter-stage fan-out/fan-in.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Cooperative-yield workloads: outside the formal scope.}
The DTA algorithm targets spawn-and-complete tasks; it does not specify
a yield execution model.  The \dtact{} implementation uses stackful fibres,
which causes \texttt{yield\_now()} to traverse the full spawn path
(Remark~\ref{rem:confounders}).  This is an \dtact{} implementation
limitation, not an algorithmic one: a DTA implementation built on stackless
futures would not incur this overhead.  In either case, cooperative-yield
workloads lie outside the formal performance model of this paper.

\subsection{Load regime}

The stability ladder (Performance~IV) identifies three regimes:
\begin{itemize}
  \item $\varrho_0 < 1$: throughput-stable (Level~1 guarantee).
        At high load ($\varrho_0 \to 1^-$), deflection is most
        beneficial: imbalance is driven to $O(1/\sqrt{N})$ and the
        system approaches full utilisation.
  \item $\varrho_0 > 1$ transiently (burst): the warehouse absorbs
        $B^* = C_W \mu$ tasks per second before overloading.
        With $H = 114{,}688$ (production config), burst tolerance
        is 573$\times$ higher than at the benchmark $H=200$.
  \item $\varrho_0 \gg 1$ sustained: instability is inevitable for
        any finite-capacity scheduler.  The first-passage crash time
        $E[\tau_{\mathrm{crash}}]$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:crash-time})
        quantifies the safety margin.
\end{itemize}
The main lesson is that \textbf{DTA is most advantageous at high but
subcritical load} ($\varrho_0 \in [0.6, 0.95]$): deflection actively
reduces imbalance, the makespan bound is tight, and the warehouse
provides a substantial burst buffer.

\subsection{Hardware topology}

The formal model assumes a $K_4$ all-to-all SPSC topology.
The hash-routing Proposition~\ref{prop:hash_mf} shows this is exact
under uniform flow IDs.  For NUMA or CCX-topology hardware, the
mean-field result gives the topology-unaware baseline; same-socket
peer preference is an $O(1/N)$ perturbation.  In practice, for
$N \leq 8$ workers pinned to a single socket, all-to-all latency is
small ($\leq 30\,\mathrm{ns}$ SPSC write) and the topology correction
is negligible.

For larger multi-socket deployments, Part~VI (\S\ref{sec:numa_concrete})
provides a quantitative framework.  On a representative 64-core dual-socket
system, DTA's information-acquisition cost $\beta_{\mathrm{DTA}}$ is
$\approx 9.9\times$ lower than WS's $\beta_{\mathrm{WS}}$ at $N=64$,
growing as $\Omega(\log N)$.  The crossover threshold $\gamma^* \to 0$
as $W \to \infty$, confirming that DTA is the preferred choice at high
task counts on NUMA hardware.

% ============================================================
\section{Summary of Contributions}
\label{sec:contributions}
% ============================================================

We summarise the principal contributions of this paper.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Methodological contributions.}
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Operator-algebraic scheduler modelling.}
        A Fock-space formalism for concurrent task schedulers that reduces
        correctness proofs to algebraic commutation identities, independent
        of task count.
  \item \textbf{BBGKY expansion for distributed schedulers.}
        First systematic application of the BBGKY kinetic hierarchy to
        a work-stealing / deflection scheduler, yielding a rigorous
        $O(1/N)$ correction to the mean-field prediction.
  \item \textbf{Kinetic-theory framework for DAG scheduling.}
        A mean-field ODE describing layer-completion fractions in
        fork-join DAGs, with two provable second-order corrections
        ($\delta^{(A)}$ Gumbel fluctuation; $\delta^{(B)}$ incomplete
        relaxation via Fill's TV-distance bound) and a directed-percolation
        phase transition identified as an open problem.
  \item \textbf{Adversarial worst-case BoT bound without mean-field.}
        A competitive-ratio upper bound whose proof is derived purely
        from work conservation and hop-count geometry and requires no
        probabilistic assumption on the arrival sequence (its stability
        precondition $\varrho_0 < 1$ is separately established via the
        mean-field analysis of Performance~IV).
  \item \textbf{Information-theoretic scheduler cost framework.}
        A formal decomposition of the competitive ratio into information
        gap and utilisation efficiency; a proof that both
        $\eta_{\mathrm{util}} \to 1$ and $\eta_{\mathrm{info}} \to 1$ for
        DTA as $W \to \infty$ (heavy-traffic information-optimality); and a
        quantitative $\Omega(\log N)$ cost advantage over WS on NUMA
        topologies, with a practitioner methodology for computing the
        DTA/WS crossover threshold $\gamma^*$ on target hardware.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Analytical results for DTA.}
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Four correctness theorems: task conservation, deadlock freedom,
        livelock freedom ($\leq\lfloor N/2\rfloor$ hops), and
        linearizability of SPSC and MPMC queues.
  \item Bounded waiting time theorem with explicit, distribution-free
        closed-form ceiling $W_{\max}(N,L,C_W,K,\delta)$ and $k$-bounded
        fairness corollary.
  \item Mean-field load balance: M/M/1/$H$ self-consistency equation,
        queue-length imbalance $O(1/\sqrt{N})$ under deflection,
        propagation-of-chaos validation for $N \to \infty$.
  \item Four-level stability ladder: Foster--Lyapunov throughput stability,
        NESS convergence rate (spectral gap $\gamma \geq \mu(1-\varrho^*)$),
        adversarial burst absorption ($B^* \propto NH\mu/\lambda$),
        and crash-free first-passage threshold.
  \item Two-line makespan bound: deterministic work-conservation term
        $T_1/N + \delta_{\mathrm{hop}}\cdot\lfloor N/2\rfloor$
        plus Gumbel fluctuation correction $O(\log N/\mu)$.
  \item Empirical validation: all predictions confirmed within
        Criterion confidence intervals across seven benchmark categories
        on GitHub Actions CI.
  \item Statistical moment analysis: variance, MAD, and CV compared
        against Gumbel-predicted standard deviation; observed runner
        noise 847--433{,}000$\times$ larger than theoretical Gumbel
        fluctuation; Tokio CV plateau at large $M$ identified as an
        allocator-contention signature.
\end{enumerate}

% ============================================================
\section{Future Work and Open Problems}
\label{sec:future_work}
% ============================================================

We close with the most promising open directions, ordered from nearest
to most speculative.

\subsection{Near-term: engineering extensions}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Yield-path optimisation.}
The 4.7$\times$ yield regression (Remark~\ref{rem:confounders}) is an
engineering, not a theoretical, limitation.  A dedicated
\emph{local re-enqueue} path that bypasses the SPSC mailbox for
\texttt{yield\_now()} within the same worker thread would close the
gap with Tokio's ring-buffer fast path.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{NUMA-aware peer selection.}
Extending the hash-routing Proposition~\ref{prop:hash_mf} to
non-uniform topologies: replace the uniform peer distribution with
a NUMA-distance-weighted kernel and re-derive the BBGKY Level-1
correction.  This requires solving a weighted self-consistency equation
in place of~\eqref{eq:sc-mf}.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Adaptive threshold $H$.}
The current model treats $H$ as a static parameter.  A dynamic
threshold (e.g.\ $H(t) = H_0 + \kappa \cdot \bar{q}(t)$) could
improve burst absorption while preserving throughput stability;
the four-level ladder would need to be re-derived with a
time-dependent Lyapunov function.

\subsection{Medium-term: theoretical extensions}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Level-2 BBGKY correction for load balance.}
\hyperref[chap:kinetic_theory]{Performance~II} closes the hierarchy at
level~1, whose residual error we now show (in this version) is
$O(1/N^2)$ \emph{in order} via a general propagation-of-chaos scaling
argument (Proposition~\ref{prop:residual-order}), without deriving the
triplet correlator $g_3(q,q',q'')$ explicitly. The remaining open
problem is therefore narrower than before: derive $g_3$'s explicit
functional form (not merely its order), which by the same scaling
argument would let a level-2 closure reduce the residual further, from
$O(1/N^2)$ to $O(1/N^3)$, and would additionally pin down the
$O(1/N^2)$ constant that is currently only illustrated
order-of-magnitude (\S\ref{chap:kinetic_theory}) rather than derived
in closed form.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Large-deviation theory for makespan tails.}
The current makespan bound (Performance~III) gives an expected value
and a sub-exponential concentration inequality.  A large-deviation
analysis~\cite{gardiner2004} of the form
$P(C_{\max} > c) \leq \exp(-\Omega(c \cdot N \mu))$ would provide
tail bounds useful for real-time scheduling guarantees.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Non-Poisson arrival processes.}
The mean-field analysis assumes Poisson arrivals (M/M/1/$H$).
Extensions to Markov-modulated Poisson processes (MMPP) or heavy-tailed
inter-arrival distributions would require replacing the Boltzmann
collision integral with a more general L\'evy-process generator.

\subsection{Long-term: open problems}

The two open problems identified in the DAG kinetic theory
(\S\ref{sec:dag_kinetic}) remain the most mathematically challenging:

\medskip
\noindent\textit{Open Problem~1 (DP universality for DAG scheduling).}
Establish rigorously that the scheduling phase transition in the random
DAG ensemble is in the directed-percolation (DP) universality class,
and that the makespan scaling exponents equal the DP critical exponents
$\beta \approx 0.2765$, $\nu_\parallel \approx 1.734$,
$\nu_\perp \approx 1.097$~\cite{hinrichsen2000}.
A proof would require controlling the propagation of chaos through the
DAG depth, likely using the Sznitman coupling~\cite{sznitman1991}
extended to tree-structured particle systems.

\medskip
\noindent\textit{Open Problem~2 (BBGKY Level-2 for DAG kinetics).}
Derive the level-2 BBGKY correction to the DAG ODE~\eqref{eq:dag_ode},
closing at the triplet-correlator level.  This would yield the first
fluctuation correction to the DP mean-field exponents and provide a
controlled perturbative route to the full universality-class analysis.

\medskip
\noindent\textit{Open Problem~3 (operator algebra for non-conservative systems).}
The Fock-space formalism is exact for conservative systems (tasks are
conserved).  Extending it to \emph{task creation} (e.g.\ recursive
spawning) or \emph{task cancellation} requires a generalised number
operator with a source term; the commutativity proof would need to track
net flux rather than strict conservation.

\medskip
\noindent\textit{Open Problem~4 (DTA on heterogeneous NUMA topologies).}
Modern multi-socket servers expose \emph{non-uniform memory access} (NUMA)
costs: the SPSC latency $\delta(i,j)$ between workers $i$ and $j$ depends
on their physical placement, breaking the homogeneity assumption that
underlies all mean-field results of this paper.  We lay a group-theoretic
foundation and state the central open question.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Topological group structure.}
Model the $N$ workers as elements of a finite abelian group
\begin{equation}\label{eq:numa_group}
  G \;=\; \mathbb{Z}_{N_s} \times \mathbb{Z}_{N_c},
\end{equation}
where $N_s$ is the number of NUMA sockets and $N_c = N/N_s$ the number of
cores per socket.  The \emph{Cayley graph} $\mathrm{Cay}(G,S)$ with generator
set $S = \{(\pm 1, 0),\, (0, \pm 1)\}$ encodes worker adjacency; it is a
discrete $2$-torus with the lattice structure familiar from condensed matter
physics.  The intra-socket subgroup
\[
  H \;=\; \{0\} \times \mathbb{Z}_{N_c} \;\leq\; G
\]
acts as a translation symmetry: any two workers in the same socket are
related by an element of $H$, and the quotient $G/H \cong \mathbb{Z}_{N_s}$
indexes the sockets.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{NUMA latency kernel and $H$-bi-invariance.}
Define the SPSC latency function $\delta \colon G \times G \to \mathbb{R}_{+}$
by
\begin{equation}\label{eq:numa_delta}
  \delta(g,h) \;=\;
  \begin{cases}
    \delta_{\mathrm{intra}} & \text{if } gh^{-1} \in H
                              \quad (\text{same socket}), \\
    \delta_{\mathrm{inter}} & \text{otherwise}
                              \quad (\text{cross-socket}),
  \end{cases}
\end{equation}
with $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}} > \delta_{\mathrm{intra}} > 0$.
This kernel is $H$-bi-invariant: $\delta(kg, kh) = \delta(g,h)$ for all
$k \in H$, reflecting the fact that intra-socket latency is independent of
which specific cores within a socket are involved.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Group-indexed BBGKY density and Fourier decomposition.}
In the NUMA regime the one-body density is a \emph{group-indexed field}
$f_1^g(q,t)$ --- the marginal queue-length distribution of worker $g$.
Under the $H$-bi-invariance of~\eqref{eq:numa_delta}, $f_1^g$ depends on
$g$ only through its coset $gH \in G/H$, yielding $N_s$ coupled mean-field
equations (one per socket) in place of the single equation~\eqref{eq:sc-mf}.

To analyse inter-socket imbalance we expand in the irreducible
representations (irreps) of $G$.  Since $G$ is abelian, every irrep is a
one-dimensional character
\[
  \chi_{(k,\ell)}(s,c) \;=\; \omega_{N_s}^{ks}\,\omega_{N_c}^{\ell c},
  \qquad
  \omega_n \;=\; e^{2\pi i/n},
\]
indexed by $(k,\ell) \in \hat G \cong G$.  Define the discrete Fourier
coefficient
\begin{equation}\label{eq:numa_fourier}
  \hat{F}_{(k,\ell)}(q,t)
  \;=\;
  \frac{1}{|G|}\sum_{g \in G} f_1^g(q,t)\,\overline{\chi_{(k,\ell)}(g)}.
\end{equation}
The \emph{zero mode} $(k,\ell) = (0,0)$ recovers the socket-averaged density
$\bar f_1(q,t)$, which satisfies the standard mean-field equation derived in
\S\ref{chap:kinetic_theory}.  Non-zero modes quantify inter- and intra-socket imbalance;
their amplitudes $|\hat{F}_{(k,\ell)}|$ measure the deviation from the
homogeneous mean-field prediction.  The spectral gap
$\lambda_1 = \min_{(k,\ell)\neq(0,0)} \hat\delta(k,\ell)$
of the latency kernel (Fourier-transformed via the same formula) controls
whether imbalance modes decay or persist at stationarity.

\medskip
\noindent\textit{Open question.}
Prove (or disprove) that the stationary distribution of the group-indexed
BBGKY Level-1 system admits a Fourier-mode decoupling analogous
to~\eqref{eq:numa_fourier}, and determine the effect of the explicit
symmetry breaking $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}} > \delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$ on
the universality class identified in Open Problem~1: does the directed-percolation
exponent $\beta \approx 0.2765$ survive, or does a new universality class
emerge when the ratio $\delta_{\mathrm{inter}}/\delta_{\mathrm{intra}}$
exceeds a critical threshold?



\bigskip
\noindent\textbf{Summary.}
We set out to answer whether restricting a distributed scheduler to
bounded, local information is a free design choice or a real cost. The
answer, for the DTA scheduler studied here, is that it is close to free:
DTA is simultaneously \emph{provably correct} (four theorems), \emph{provably
live} (bounded wait, starvation freedom), \emph{quantitatively
characterised} (mean-field + BBGKY + stability ladder + makespan) at the
same leading order as schedulers with a wider information window,
\emph{competitively near-optimal} (adversarial ratio $\to 1$ at high load),
and \emph{empirically validated} against public CI benchmarks --- while
provably paying a strictly lower, asymptotically $\Omega(\log N)$ smaller
information-acquisition cost than work-stealing on NUMA hardware
(Part~VI). The kinetic-theory and operator-algebraic methods introduced
here are general-purpose tools for distributed-systems analysis and are
expected to find application well beyond the specific scheduler studied;
the information-theoretic framework of Part~VI in particular gives a
reusable methodology for deciding, on any target hardware, whether a
bounded-information scheduler like DTA or a wider-information scheduler
like work-stealing is the better choice.

\clearpage

% ============================================================
\section{Method}
\label{sec:ai-method}
% ============================================================

ACM's current policy on generative AI does not require disclosure of AI
assistance used in writing the manuscript itself; disclosure is required
for AI used \emph{in the conduct of the research}. This section records
the latter: the role of AI assistance in producing the artifacts this
paper's claims depend on --- the simulation/validation scripts and the
\dtact{} reference implementation --- together with the human review each
artifact received before being relied upon in this paper. All
mathematical derivations, theorem statements, and proofs are the
responsibility of the human author.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Figures and simulation scripts.}
The Monte Carlo, ODE, and finite-size simulation scripts in the
\texttt{script/} directory that generate the figures throughout this
paper (mean-field self-consistency solvers, CTMC simulators, DAG
scheduling simulations, and the FSM verifier of
\S\ref{chap:formal_model}) were developed with AI assistance and
subsequently reviewed by the author for correctness of the underlying
model against the formal definitions of Part~I and against the numerical
predictions of the corresponding theorems. Where a script's parameters
are scaled down from the production configuration for computational
tractability (e.g.\ $N$, $H$ in several Performance-chapter simulations),
this is noted in the script itself and, where relevant, in the
surrounding text.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{\dtact{} implementation.}
The reference implementation of the DTA scheduler (\texttt{dta\_scheduler.rs}
and supporting modules in the \dtact{} repository~\cite{dtact}) that this
paper analyses was developed with AI assistance and subsequently reviewed
by the author, including a manual audit of every atomic operation's
memory-ordering annotation against the requirements established in
\S\ref{sec:mpmc}--\S\ref{sec:memorder}. Responsibility for the
correctness of the implementation, and for the accuracy of its
correspondence to the formal model, rests with the human author.
